<span id="freeTextContainer10101840727025407118">In the summer of 1984, the war in Vietnam comes home to Sam Hughes, whose father was killed there before she was born: The soldier-boy in the picture never changed. In a way that made him dependable. But he seemed so innocent.<br /><br />"Astronauts have been to the moon," she blurted out to the picture. "You missed Watergate. I was in the second grade."<br /><br />She stared at the picture, squi</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10101840727025407118">In the summer of 1984, the war in Vietnam comes home to Sam Hughes, whose father was killed there before she was born: The soldier-boy in the picture never changed. In a way that made him dependable. But he seemed so innocent.<br /><br />"Astronauts have been to the moon," she blurted out to the picture. "You missed Watergate. I was in the second grade."<br /><br />She stared at the picture, squinting her eyes, as if she expected it to come to life. But Dwayne had died with his secrets. Emmett was walking around with his. Anyone who survived Vietnam seemed to regard it as something personal and embarrassing. Granddad had said they were embarrassed that they lost the war, but Emmett said they were embarrassed that they were still alive. "I guess you' re not embarrassed," she said to the picture.</span>

      <a href="/user/show/2732767-adam" class="user" title="Adam">Adam</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer87988659">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1696079475894388119">Unless the reviewers have a deep personal connection with the main character's situation, I can really see no justification for saying this is by any means a good book. <br />The dialog is horrible and robotic.<br />The pop-culture references are forced, overabundant, and presented poorly. (i.e. "It sounds like the domino theory...I read about the domino theory in school." The author gives no description of what the domino theory is, so it seems understood that the theory is common knowledge, but then the m</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1696079475894388119">Unless the reviewers have a deep personal connection with the main character's situation, I can really see no justification for saying this is by any means a good book. <br />The dialog is horrible and robotic.<br />The pop-culture references are forced, overabundant, and presented poorly. (i.e. "It sounds like the domino theory...I read about the domino theory in school." The author gives no description of what the domino theory is, so it seems understood that the theory is common knowledge, but then the main character feels the need to explain where she heard about it. A pop-culture consuming teen in 1984 only knew what the domino theory was because of school?)<br />Name-dropping Bruce Springsteen every other page isn't going to make the reader feel like they're in the 80's, it's just annoying.<br />Some of the writing is outright shitty.<br />This is all forgivable if the story is enticing or entertaining or thoughtful or provocative or has any redeeming qualities. Too bad there's little if any conflict, main characters who many people will hate even though they're not meant to be anti-hero, and barely any emotion.</span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer382601396">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1883601545124227406">Very much enjoyed this book. It was a "blast from the past," as we used to say growing up. Set in 1984, many of the references and allusions are to music/things/products/foods that were part of my youth. I was roughly the same age as the main character in 1984, in fact, so for sure could relate.<br /><br />The book details (almost overloads you, in fact) on the repercussions of the Vietnam War. For readers who didn't grow up during this time, the subject is still timely, considering all wars have similar af</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1883601545124227406">Very much enjoyed this book. It was a "blast from the past," as we used to say growing up. Set in 1984, many of the references and allusions are to music/things/products/foods that were part of my youth. I was roughly the same age as the main character in 1984, in fact, so for sure could relate.<br /><br />The book details (almost overloads you, in fact) on the repercussions of the Vietnam War. For readers who didn't grow up during this time, the subject is still timely, considering all wars have similar after effects for friends and families of the soldiers who return home (or who don't). One of the most powerful passages is when Sam looks at the photo of her father, who died in the war:<br /><br />"She stared at the picture...But Dwayne had died with his secrets. Emmett was walking around with his. Anyone who survived Vietnam seemed to regard it as something personal and embarrassing. Granddad had said they were embarrassed that they lost the war, but Emmett said they were embarrassed that they were still alive. 'I guess you're not embarrassed,' she said to the picture.<br /><br />"The face in the picture ruled the room, like the picture of the President on the wall of the high school auditorium. Sam set Sgt. Pepper's Lonely Hearts Club Band on the stereo.<br /><br />"'You missed this too,' she said."</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1883601545124227406">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_339742087">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/339742087" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4728532-stacy-pershall" class="left imgcol" title="Stacy Pershall"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442519843p2/4728532.jpg" alt="Stacy Pershall" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/339742087?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 29, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4728532-stacy-pershall" class="user" title="Stacy Pershall">Stacy Pershall</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/81648.In_Country" title="Paperback 9780060835170" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer339742087">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2892210644944799128">As someone obsessed with Vietnam, the '80s, and strange-girls-coming-of-age stories, I was keen to read this one. And for the first 3/4 of the book, it didn't disappoint. Great heroine, set in the South, lots of references to early MTV, and a gripping central mystery: are Uncle Emmett's health problems a result of Agent Orange or not? It definitely kept me turning pages, and more than once I was reminded of Carson McCullers (always a good thing.)<br /><br />But then it came to the last 40 or so pages, and i</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2892210644944799128">As someone obsessed with Vietnam, the '80s, and strange-girls-coming-of-age stories, I was keen to read this one. And for the first 3/4 of the book, it didn't disappoint. Great heroine, set in the South, lots of references to early MTV, and a gripping central mystery: are Uncle Emmett's health problems a result of Agent Orange or not? It definitely kept me turning pages, and more than once I was reminded of Carson McCullers (always a good thing.)<br /><br />But then it came to the last 40 or so pages, and it fell into hackneyed dialogue and sloppy, sentimental writing. Characters went "weak in the knees," were "wracked with sobs," and planes had wings like birds (Uncle Emmett's a bird watcher, and the symbolism gets ham-fisted as all hell.) It was like she had to finish it in a hurry and without an editor or something. Way too many words that didn't need to be there -- for example, a character's talking, the quotation marks end in the middle of a paragraph, and then there's a "He continued," more quotes, and more of the character's speech. I found myself editing as I read, which is never a good sign.<br /><br />It's the first Bobbie Ann Mason I've read, and I liked the parts I liked well enough to read something else she wrote. I'll certainly give her another chance, I just wonder what happened here.<br /><br />Oh, and don't expect the mystery to be solved.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2892210644944799128">...more</a>

          </span>

  <a href="/user/show/2004692-anne" class="left imgcol" title="Anne"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" alt="Anne" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/45633178?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 08, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2004692-anne" class="user" title="Anne">Anne</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/81648.In_Country" title="Paperback 9780060835170" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer45633178">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4074260915604872196">This book has stuck with me since I first read it ten years ago in an American Literature college course. It's a book that, stylistically, probably deserves four stars; there are some awkward jolts in the momentum of the story. But I can't bring myself to lower my perfect rating. I get so attached to Sam and Emmett everytime I re-read this book that I feel like I would be letting them down personally if I were to confess flaws in the story. <br />Several reviewers have noted that this book ought to be</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4074260915604872196">This book has stuck with me since I first read it ten years ago in an American Literature college course. It's a book that, stylistically, probably deserves four stars; there are some awkward jolts in the momentum of the story. But I can't bring myself to lower my perfect rating. I get so attached to Sam and Emmett everytime I re-read this book that I feel like I would be letting them down personally if I were to confess flaws in the story. <br />Several reviewers have noted that this book ought to be on high school reading lists. I agree. As a high school English teacher, I added this to my curriculum and found it was a great asset, both to class conversation, but also to my students' understanding of Vietnam and its legacy. Mason deftly juxtaposes the vapid pop culture of 1980's America against the raw wound of Vietnam's collective memory. </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_138918744">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/138918744" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1077703-ed" class="left imgcol" title="Ed"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442473851p2/1077703.jpg" alt="Ed" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/138918744?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 10, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1077703-ed" class="user" title="Ed">Ed</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer138918744">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17362601952044386796">Sensitive, insightful novel about the Vietnam War published in 1985 amid the tide of fiction on the topic being written then. I reviewed many of them. Here I liked the articulate voice of the narrator, Sam Hughes, whose father died over there and whose vet uncle she lives with in Kentucky. Sam is learning about life and herself. Lots of pop culture references from the time period are included. If I had to read one work of fiction on Vietnam, then I'd pick this one. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

      <a href="/user/show/944123-alisa" class="user" title="Alisa">Alisa</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/81648.In_Country" title="Paperback 9780060835170" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/944123-alisa?shelf=audiobooks" class="actionLinkLite">audiobooks</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/944123-alisa?shelf=book-club" class="actionLinkLite">book-club</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1032111995">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12427799895421679462">It's pretty rare a book turns me around after underwhelming me a lot at the start, but this one did. Abby rightly pointed out Bobbie Ann Mason writes a mean short story, and in some ways each chapter of this novel feels like a story, some stronger than others. My interest in fiction related to the Vietnam War is zero; hippies make me crazy and I never want to read books about political activism of any kind, but Mason here keeps it tightly focused on one haphazard family and their small circle of</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12427799895421679462">It's pretty rare a book turns me around after underwhelming me a lot at the start, but this one did. Abby rightly pointed out Bobbie Ann Mason writes a mean short story, and in some ways each chapter of this novel feels like a story, some stronger than others. My interest in fiction related to the Vietnam War is zero; hippies make me crazy and I never want to read books about political activism of any kind, but Mason here keeps it tightly focused on one haphazard family and their small circle of veteran friends. Sam, the teenage narrator on the cusp of adulthood, is sympathetic enough and everyone else is engaging and resist becoming caricatures of Kentuckians or troubled vets. But the present tense/flashback structure is a pretty glaring weakness; the flashback section (the majority of the novel) is much stronger than its beginning and end.<br /><br />The audiobook read by Jill Brennan is deplorable in every way. Her Southern accent and voice modulation made me cringe throughout.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_176335641">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/176335641" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5567347-craig-werner" class="left imgcol" title="Craig Werner"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442457485p2/5567347.jpg" alt="Craig Werner" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/176335641?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 05, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5567347-craig-werner" class="user" title="Craig Werner">Craig Werner</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/81648.In_Country" title="Paperback 9780060835170" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/5567347-craig-werner?shelf=contemporary-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">contemporary-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5567347-craig-werner?shelf=vietnam" class="actionLinkLite">vietnam</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer176335641">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13363619575428436979">My reaction on re-reading's pretty much what it was the first time through. Mason's telling an important story focused on the daughter of a father killed in Vietnam before her birth. Sam Hughes, the daughter, is immersed in mid-80s pop culture (especially Bruce Springsteen's Born in the USA album and MASH re-runs) and the sketch of the small-town Kentucky milieu is compelling but not as densely realized as Mason's short story collections. It's the classic "nothing major wrong with it" book that</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13363619575428436979">My reaction on re-reading's pretty much what it was the first time through. Mason's telling an important story focused on the daughter of a father killed in Vietnam before her birth. Sam Hughes, the daughter, is immersed in mid-80s pop culture (especially Bruce Springsteen's Born in the USA album and MASH re-runs) and the sketch of the small-town Kentucky milieu is compelling but not as densely realized as Mason's short story collections. It's the classic "nothing major wrong with it" book that doesn't take me anywhere I'm not already aware of. The community of Vietnam vets tilts a bit toward "types" (not quite stereotypes); the agent orange theme is praiseworthy but a bit superficial; Sam's reading of her fathers letters and diary leads to an emotional response which doesn't quite work--it requires us to believe she's much more naive about the war than someone who's read a bunch of books about it, which she has, could possibly remain. <br /><br />One specific false note: Sam's uncle Emmett is one of the anti-war vets portrayed in the novel. At one point, he unfurls a Viet Cong flag as a political statement. While I know a good number of anti-war Vietnam vets, I've never met one who would have considered associating himself with the Viet Cong or the NVA flags, which represent sympathy for those who killed their comrades. Placing the scene in a small Southern town makes it even less believable.<br /><br />An okay introduction to an important set of themes. The book teaches well, but has to be followed up with some serious footnoting.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13363619575428436979">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_137659729">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/137659729" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/447506-curt-bozif" class="left imgcol" title="Curt Bozif"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1410986784p2/447506.jpg" alt="Curt Bozif" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/137659729?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 01, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/447506-curt-bozif" class="user" title="Curt Bozif">Curt Bozif</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/447506-curt-bozif?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/447506-curt-bozif?shelf=vietnam" class="actionLinkLite">vietnam</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/447506-curt-bozif?shelf=war" class="actionLinkLite">war</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer137659729">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14060259846031342694">This book was written at a strange time in America's relationship to the Vietnam War. Reagan was president, and people had stopped asking "why were we in Vietnam?" and started asking an even stranger question, "why didn't we win in Vietnam?" The National Vietnam Veterans Memorial had just been dedicated and the country was supposedly in the process of finally welcoming home it's forgotten veterans. But for my taste, In Country is often times heavy handed, preachy, and melodramatic. The dialogue</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14060259846031342694">This book was written at a strange time in America's relationship to the Vietnam War. Reagan was president, and people had stopped asking "why were we in Vietnam?" and started asking an even stranger question, "why didn't we win in Vietnam?" The National Vietnam Veterans Memorial had just been dedicated and the country was supposedly in the process of finally welcoming home it's forgotten veterans. But for my taste, In Country is often times heavy handed, preachy, and melodramatic. The dialogue seems unrealistic. But, despite it's limitations I know of no other book like this one. In Country provides a unique perspective on complexities of not only growing up a child of a Vietnam vet, but a daughter of a Vietnam vet, which I've seen first hand with my older sister, but can in no way completely understand.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14060259846031342694">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1064770047">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1064770047" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/34764374-cameron-stuart" class="left imgcol" title="Cameron Stuart"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1410189073p2/34764374.jpg" alt="Cameron Stuart" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1064770047?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 26, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/34764374-cameron-stuart" class="user" title="Cameron Stuart">Cameron Stuart</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/81648.In_Country" title="Paperback 9780060835170" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1064770047">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7634659245428236243">With an unconventional family structure, carefully worked popular culture references and Vietnam Vet's that are a little more three dimensional than the stock "good guy goes to 'nam, it's horrible and he comes back scarred and angry," Mason creates a coming-of-age tale not just of Sam and Emmett, but of a distinct national experience. The story does tend to clunk along like Sam's shitty VW on its way to D.C, but "In Country" is an enjoyable read and a refreshing take on post-Vietnam America. The</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7634659245428236243">With an unconventional family structure, carefully worked popular culture references and Vietnam Vet's that are a little more three dimensional than the stock "good guy goes to 'nam, it's horrible and he comes back scarred and angry," Mason creates a coming-of-age tale not just of Sam and Emmett, but of a distinct national experience. The story does tend to clunk along like Sam's shitty VW on its way to D.C, but "In Country" is an enjoyable read and a refreshing take on post-Vietnam America. The continual transition of America from small settlements and community spirit into a commercial behemoth is nicely juxtaposed with the neglect and decline of the Vietnam Veteran and his experience. I recommend listening to Springsteen's "Born in the USA" album in its intended entirety pre-read; it is referenced several times during the novel and provides a fitting backdrop of the era, war experience and importance of locale in the text. A great story hindered slightly by its labored tempo. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7634659245428236243">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <a href="/user/show/2127386-anesa" class="user" title="Anesa">Anesa</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer823637242">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12364090405075728025">Mason's major novel concerns a high school senior named Samantha whose father died very young in Viet Nam, never having seen his infant daughter. Samantha's mother has recently fled small-town Kentucky after mothering her own brother, an alienated Viet Nam vet, for many years. Sam now lives with this unfortunate uncle and attempts to fill her mother's vacated shoes.<br /><br />Most of the book is devoted to Sam's efforts at getting any- and everyone around her to open up about the war experience. These pass</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12364090405075728025">Mason's major novel concerns a high school senior named Samantha whose father died very young in Viet Nam, never having seen his infant daughter. Samantha's mother has recently fled small-town Kentucky after mothering her own brother, an alienated Viet Nam vet, for many years. Sam now lives with this unfortunate uncle and attempts to fill her mother's vacated shoes.<br /><br />Most of the book is devoted to Sam's efforts at getting any- and everyone around her to open up about the war experience. These passages are moving and effective. The final 30 pages seemed to me a bit forced as we move toward a crisis/gestalt moment in which Sam attempts to relive a semblance of her father's wartime experience. Nonetheless, I liked the shifty narrative, combining 3rd- and 1st-person points of view. I felt this brought the characters to life in an unusual way.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12364090405075728025">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer17699550838405048170">So very OK. Probably my own Northeast liberal bias coming through, but between that and knowing exactly four people who served in Vietnam, I found it much more interesting to look at the book as a cultural study of the Midwest than anything else. The only likeable character was Everett, but even he lapses into what is now almost a caricature of PTSD/the tortured vet. I just feel bad for Sam, whose idea of excitement seems to be hitting the mall. I think chasing <u>The Bean Trees</u> with this may have</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17699550838405048170">So very OK. Probably my own Northeast liberal bias coming through, but between that and knowing exactly four people who served in Vietnam, I found it much more interesting to look at the book as a cultural study of the Midwest than anything else. The only likeable character was Everett, but even he lapses into what is now almost a caricature of PTSD/the tortured vet. I just feel bad for Sam, whose idea of excitement seems to be hitting the mall. I think chasing <u>The Bean Trees</u> with this may have been a mistake, since they're both written in the same voice, a teenage/young adult woman from the sticks whose primary short-term goal is not getting pregnant.</span>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1269515864">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14789220690007927107">Bret Easton Ellis was 21 when <em>Less Than Zero</em> was published and Jay McInerney 34 when <em>Bright Lights, Big City</em> came out. Bobbie Ann Mason, by contrast, was already 45 when her first novel, <em>In Country</em>, was released in 1985, following a successful collection of short stories. Being older probably gave her a head start on reflecting on the human condition, since she'd had some more of it in her life.<br /><br />In the summer of 1984, a year of Springsteen and Michael Jackson, Samantha "Sam" Hughes graduates from</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14789220690007927107">Bret Easton Ellis was 21 when <em>Less Than Zero</em> was published and Jay McInerney 34 when <em>Bright Lights, Big City</em> came out. Bobbie Ann Mason, by contrast, was already 45 when her first novel, <em>In Country</em>, was released in 1985, following a successful collection of short stories. Being older probably gave her a head start on reflecting on the human condition, since she'd had some more of it in her life.<br /><br />In the summer of 1984, a year of Springsteen and Michael Jackson, Samantha "Sam" Hughes graduates from Hopewell High School in Kentucky. Her father died in Vietnam before she was born. Her mother has recently remarried and moved to a nearby city. Sam and her uncle, Emmett Smith, still live in Hopewell, but her mother wants her to move in with her so she can attend college. Sam wonders who will take care of Emmett, the only real father figure she has ever known but who came back from his own Vietnam experience damaged enough for her to be unsure of his ability to take care of himself.<br /><br />Through the summer, Sam tries to learn about the war that took her father's life as well as understand something about what she wants in the world. Does she want to stay in Hopewell to care for Emmett and probably marry her boyfriend Lonnie (locally famous for sinking a string of jump shots during a senior season basketball game)? The placing of the Vietnam War Memorial wall in Washington, D.C., provides a spur for both her and Emmett to seek their answers.<br /><br />As an exploration of character and condition, <em>In Country</em> stands well above either <em>Zero</em> or <em>Lights</em>. It lacks the former's poisonous indifference towards its own characters and the latter's arty affectations, and it roots its meditations and narrative in experiences much more common and relatable than the lurid revelries of the American coasts. I read <em>In Country</em> and I want to find out what Sam will learn and what Emmett will do -- none of the cast of either of the other two novels evokes that response. Ellis doesn't care enough about his to give them humanity and McInerney doesn't care enough about his to give his narrator a name, and if they don't care, why should I?<br /><br />Like Clay and "you," Sam learns that she wants answers beyond what she's always known. But unlike Clay, she takes steps to find them, and unlike "you," she doesn't run from them and hide behind a mound of white powder. Is that because Mason had enough perspective to look at that verge-of-adulthood time of life with a clearer eye that the others, who were too close to really see it? Maybe. Either way, she produced the best book of the three, and the least famous. Oh well.<br /><br />Original available <a rel="nofollow" href="http://friarsfires.blogspot.com/2013/08/flashback-trio.html">here</a>.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer2327857578142322802">In Country by Bobbie Ann Mason<br /><br />I already knew this was a five star book when I started reading it. I'd read it a long time ago before I started doing book reviews to keep me from selecting books I'd already read from the library. I have only one shelf in my home library dedicated to fiction I've already read and In Country was one of those few selected books that I had to keep. The shelf is full of memorable books that were either super enjoyable, extremely well written, inspiring or just hit so</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2327857578142322802">In Country by Bobbie Ann Mason<br /><br />I already knew this was a five star book when I started reading it. I'd read it a long time ago before I started doing book reviews to keep me from selecting books I'd already read from the library. I have only one shelf in my home library dedicated to fiction I've already read and In Country was one of those few selected books that I had to keep. The shelf is full of memorable books that were either super enjoyable, extremely well written, inspiring or just hit so close to home. This book fits in more than one of those categories.<br /><br />I'd probably not even heard much about Agent Orange or PTSD at the time Bobbie Ann Mason was writing this book which was published in 1985. Somewhere, she had experience or insight into the most very intricate oddities that Post Traumatic Stress Disorder causes. Although, Sam wants to blame all of her uncle Emmett's troubles on Agent Orange, I think a large part of what was going on in this story was PTSD. Later, toward the end, Sam mentions Post Vietnam Stress Syndrome, as she has come to know and understand and seek the answers to all her questions about her father, Vietnam and the veterans she has met.<br /><br />Samantha never met her father, a farm boy, who went to Vietnam and never came back. She grew up under the influence of her uncle Emmett, who also went to Vietnam and came back with a host of problems and no ambition to do anything about them. Sam's mother tells her little about her dad, for as she puts it, "I was married to him for a month before he left and I never saw him again."<br /><br />The summer Sam turns 18, everything is changing and she tries to grasp an understanding of the past before she can move on and decide what her future will be. Her best friend Dawn is pregnant and happy to get married and stay in Hopewell. Sam's mom, Irene has recently remarried and had a baby of her own and moved to Lexington, away from Sam and Emmett. She wants Sam to move up to Lexington and go to college now. Sam has taken an unusual interest in Emmett and his Vietnam vet friends though. She breaks up with her high school boyfriend, Lonnie and is on a mission to learn and feel what Vietnam was like and why it seems to have messed up so many lives, hers included.<br /><br />I think it must be the ultimate honor for an author to have their book made into a movie. I'd read the book In Country before seeing the movie with Bruce Willis, which I will rewatch and review too. The movie is remarkably true to the book and even more memorable.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer316450159">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4751739219977230409">Readers of Nancy Drew (and Bobbie Ann Mason's scholarly analysis of the well-read series of "girl mysteries") will recognize the underlying structure of this wonderful novel about the aftermath of Vietnam. Set in a rural Southern community, the story follows the attempt of its young heroine Sam to solve the mystery of her father, who died before she knew him, a casualty of the war. Meanwhile, her curiosity is piqued by her uncle Emmett who did return from that war, but is still haunted by it and</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4751739219977230409">Readers of Nancy Drew (and Bobbie Ann Mason's scholarly analysis of the well-read series of "girl mysteries") will recognize the underlying structure of this wonderful novel about the aftermath of Vietnam. Set in a rural Southern community, the story follows the attempt of its young heroine Sam to solve the mystery of her father, who died before she knew him, a casualty of the war. Meanwhile, her curiosity is piqued by her uncle Emmett who did return from that war, but is still haunted by it and languishes in a kind of noncommunicative and prolonged bout with post traumatic stress.<br /><br />Independent and single-minded, like Nancy, Sam looks for clues and tries to piece them together. There are photographs, letters, and mementos to collect and ponder. Eventually, she tries to simulate the experience of being "in country," spending a night in the swamp, hoping to recreate for herself the fear and bravery of the young soldier far from home who was her father.<br /><br />Finally, it is a family trip to the Vietnam War Memorial in Washington, D.C., that brings them all closer to resolution. And while it may not solve a mystery, or the conundrum of the Vietnam War itself, they are drawn closer to the enigma of human life and the need to honor the past while also letting go of it. </span>

  <a href="/user/show/16969234-brendan" class="left imgcol" title="Brendan"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" alt="Brendan" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/521576695?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 10, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/16969234-brendan" class="user" title="Brendan">Brendan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/81648.In_Country" title="Paperback 9780060835170" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer521576695">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6027030495530851714"> My reaction to the book is just what I thought it would be in terms of what it would be about. Mason writes about an 18 year old girl whose father had died before she was born in the Vietnam War. The novel takes place in a small town in Kentucky and in the time period of the mid-80’s culture. This then begins with her journey of trying to regain her past of her father from the only man that knows his secrets, Emmett.<br /><br /> I really connected to this novel well. My great-grandfather, who served in Wor</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6027030495530851714">	My reaction to the book is just what I thought it would be in terms of what it would be about. Mason writes about an 18 year old girl whose father had died before she was born in the Vietnam War. The novel takes place in a small town in Kentucky and in the time period of the mid-80’s culture. This then begins with her journey of trying to regain her past of her father from the only man that knows his secrets, Emmett.<br /><br />	I really connected to this novel well. My great-grandfather, who served in World War I, died when I was only an infant. I have been interested in war history ever since I can remember and to find out that my great-grandfather had served and not being able to have the ability to hear his stories crushed me. Luckily my great-grandmother is still with me today and has told me numerous stories of how my great-grandfather flew in the air force and fought in the war. Thinking of this connection helped me relate and get a better feel for the novel as a whole.<br /><br />	Though I adored reading this novel, I see flaws or complaints that a reader may have against the book. First, the beginning of the book starts rather slow. I can see where a reader can easily get side tracked or lose interest. I was able to stay with it because of my curiosity in the Vietnam War. Second, the story can be repetitive like effects of Agent Orange, the Beatles, and M*A*S*H* over and over. Next, the novel left a bundle of loose ends that never became fully explained. Also the writing style towards the end of the novel was a bit poor. Lastly, some of the characters seemed bland. I didn’t hate any of the characters but, I didn’t love any of them either. Other than that, the story line is great and the ending really ties the whole book together. Though the book may have some flaws, overall it is worth the time to read.<br /><br />	I would recommend this novel to a person who can be patient with reading and waits things out before it becomes interesting. Also if you are a history reader, I would defiantly recommend this because it explains the war and what it was like back in the day. However, I would not recommend this to a reader that gets bored easily or loses interest quickly. Though this novel is diifferent, you will be changed by the time you are finished.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer2070733374654958268">Not sure why, but this is my first Bobbie Ann Mason; won't be my last. <em>In Country</em> may not be perfectly crafted (I could see how one might have a few complaints, like about an incident in the last scene). But I don't have those complaints--really, I'd be hard pressed to criticize anything--maybe because I got so involved it. The issues, the setting, the characters were all really important and identifiable to me--this is the first novel I've felt completely personally connected with in quite a wh</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2070733374654958268">Not sure why, but this is my first Bobbie Ann Mason; won't be my last. <em>In Country</em> may not be perfectly crafted (I could see how one might have a few complaints, like about an incident in the last scene). But I don't have those complaints--really, I'd be hard pressed to criticize anything--maybe because I got so involved it. The issues, the setting, the characters were all really important and identifiable to me--this is the first novel I've felt completely personally connected with in quite a while. I love Sam (we have some stuff in common). Love Emmett. Love Moonpie. Love Mason's gorgeous writing. <br /><br />Here's two of the several favorite passages I marked (spoiler-free): <br /><br /><br />The baby was like a growth that had come loose, Sam thought--like a scab or a wart--and Irene carried it around with her in fascination, unable to part with it. Monkeys carried dead babies around like that. A friend of Emmett's knew a lot of dead-baby jokes, but Sam couldn't remember any she had heard. In Vietnam, mothers had carried their dead babies around with them until they began to rot. <br /><br /><br />America the beautiful. It is beautiful indeed, Sam thinks now <a title="The Road by Cormac McCarthy" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6288.The_Road">on the road</a>. The United States is so peaceful and well-organized. The farms are pretty, the interstates are pleasant. Even the strip mines are hidden behind a ridge on the parkway. It is a good country. But she keeps getting flashes of it through the eyes of a just-returned Vietnam soldier. The day they came back from Cawood's Pond, she felt she was seeing that way as they drove into town, past the rendering plant and the four-corners gas stations, and the lumberyard, and the stockyards. She didn't fit in that landscape. None of it pertained to her... </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer305154385">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17558256451445359721">2.5 stars<br /><br />Full review: <a rel="nofollow" href="http://whatstheword-saywhaaat.blogspot.com/2014/12/in-country.html" target="_blank">http://whatstheword-saywhaaat.blogspo...</a><br /><br />I originally read this book in 2012.<br /><br />I'm glad I decided to reread In Country. This time I understood Sam a little bit better. In my initial read, I found her annoying to the point of dislike. Though I still think Sam's irksome, I empathize with her search for truth. I identify with Sam's frustration as she hits brick wall after brick wall. I understand her feelings of abandonment upon her mother's remarriage, move to Lexington, and new</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17558256451445359721">2.5 stars<br /><br />Full review: <a rel="nofollow" href="http://whatstheword-saywhaaat.blogspot.com/2014/12/in-country.html" target="_blank">http://whatstheword-saywhaaat.blogspo...</a><br /><br />I originally read this book in 2012.<br /><br />I'm glad I decided to reread In Country. This time I understood Sam a little bit better. In my initial read, I found her annoying to the point of dislike. Though I still think Sam's irksome, I empathize with her search for truth. I identify with Sam's frustration as she hits brick wall after brick wall. I understand her feelings of abandonment upon her mother's remarriage, move to Lexington, and new baby. I share an affinity with Sam's restlessness and discontentment. I totally get her need to learn about her father in order to gain self-awareness. "If she couldn't know a simple fact like the source of her name, what could she know for sure?" (53). Sam's quest to ferret out answers demonstrates evolving human nature: discovery, forgiveness, reconnection, obtaining peace, and hope.<br /><br />In her effort to transport the reader back to the 1980's, Mason's excessive pop culture allusions essentially diminished the import of the reference. Sprinklings of popular culture in any book, especially historical fiction is to be expected, but to bombard a reader with pop references on every page is overkill. Though I felt the writing style suited the novel, it didn't seem to flow well; which prevented it from receiving a worthy (or 3 star) rating.</span>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer155682454">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6402854385540509568">How does one go about facing the ugly truth about ourselves and others? I actually cried both times that I read this book. A young girl's search for her father's identity as she searches for her own is so effectively told here. Mom has finally gone off to start a new life as her teenaged daughter, Sam, her war-wounded (soul-wise) brother and a paternal grandmother set off to see the Vietnam memorial in D.C. when the book opens. The story of how they ever get to the beginning of this unbalanced r</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6402854385540509568">How does one go about facing the ugly truth about ourselves and others? I actually cried both times that I read this book. A young girl's search for her father's identity as she searches for her own is so effectively told here. Mom has finally gone off to start a new life as her teenaged daughter, Sam, her war-wounded (soul-wise) brother and a paternal grandmother set off to see the Vietnam memorial in D.C. when the book opens. The story of how they ever get to the beginning of this unbalanced road trip and how it ends up healing jagged parts of them are the remainder of the novel. Her parents were only married one month before Sam's father was drafted into the Vietnam War. How can you know a photograph--a ghost? Her profoundly disabled (functionally speaking) maternal uncle and Sam (Samantha) try to find a way to get along and remember, without doing either, until forced to. This is amazing literature and humanity bared, both at their best. Please experience it for yourself.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer14593319346791696947">Sometimes I think my whole generation has a kind of PTSD. But let's call it Post Viet Nam Stress Disorder. The story of Sam and Emmett (In Country) brings out some of the pain and heartache. I couldn't put it down. Emmett, a Viet Nam war vet, says, "There's something wrong with me. I'm damaged." (p. 225) We were all damaged in a way, those who protested, those who stayed home, family members of vets, and those who went "in country." That war is still with us, isn't it?</span>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1050191810">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4760812980962182061">This book really struck a chord in me. It was a super easy read, finished over a weekend. I, personally, really related to the main character as I also have a lot of questions about the Vietnam War and how it has affected so many. I would not call this the greatest novel of all time but I sure enjoyed reading it and would recommend it to others.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer8833515381675289456">Officially, one of the worst-written books I have ever read.<br />The story itself is an interesting one, but the author and the reviews of the book after it was published are just pretentious, in my opinion. <br />The author has decided to dig into an issue such as the Vietnam war and the V Memorial immediately after it was built. However, the way in which she deals with the story is outrageous. The whole second part of the book is just as an ongoing advertisement of Pepsi, Coca-Cola, McDonald's and the ma</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8833515381675289456">Officially, one of the worst-written books I have ever read.<br />The story itself is an interesting one, but the author and the reviews of the book after it was published are just pretentious, in my opinion. <br />The author has decided to dig into an issue such as the Vietnam war and the V Memorial immediately after it was built. However, the way in which she deals with the story is outrageous. The whole second part of the book is just as an ongoing advertisement of Pepsi, Coca-Cola, McDonald's and the malls in Kentucky. It might be catchy and amusing to tell the story in that way, but not in 200 pages, 2 would have been enough for everyone to get the idea.<br />The thing that strikes me the most is what metaphors she uses to describe a war memorial comparing it to the mall in a small town who knows where at the very end of the book.<br /></span>

           <span class="uitext reviewText">People w/ PTSD, interest in Vietnam - Vietnam Vets, high tolerance to wait for the good parts</span></div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer248444288">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8191494267313270743">This is one of those books where I can over look a lot of the way it's presented and written just for one thing. In this case that one thing is the portrait of PTSD. Someone had recommended the film to me (boy, was that a trick to find)and even though some of the acting &amp; actors were terrible I was really struck by certain scenes and knew I had to pick up the book so I could have the actual written words. I have read Tim O'Brien's <em>"The Things They Carried"</em>, it is a much better book in a lot</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8191494267313270743">This is one of those books where I can over look a lot of the way it's presented and written just for one thing. In this case that one thing is the portrait of PTSD. Someone had recommended the film to me (boy, was that a trick to find)and even though some of the acting &amp; actors were terrible I was really struck by certain scenes and knew I had to pick up the book so I could have the actual written words. I have read Tim O'Brien's <em>"The Things They Carried"</em>, it is a much better book in a lot of ways and truly an amazing work but for me <em>"In Country"</em> put into words so many thoughts and feelings that for years I couldn't get across to others (No, I'm not a Vet but I find no matter where it stems from a PTSD is PTSD). It's really nice when someone "gets it". So for that reason, this goes in my favorite pile.</span>

It is a deep blow to a life already in fragments: adead-end job delivering&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;span id=\"freeText18398811852347446246\" style=\"display:none\"&gt;In Ithaca, New York, in 1982, Larry Markham awakes to discover his wife, Vicki, has taken their young son, Scott, and left him--not for the first time, possibly for the last. It is a deep blow to a life already in fragments: adead-end job delivering Wonder Bread; a strained relationship with his agingfather, a veteran of World War Two; and weekly visits to the VA hospital whereLarry, a former Army medic, leads a support group for disabled Vietnam vets.As he struggles to win Vicki back, Larry finds he is in danger of a far moreimminent sort: A disturbed member of the support group--a trained CIAassassin--has disappeared, and is stalking Larry and his family. His methodssend an unmistakable message: The game will end in death.At the same time, &amp;quot;The Names of the Dead&amp;quot; is a harrowing and heartfeltportrait of the Vietnam War and the men who fought it. The year is 1968, theplace A Shau valley, and Larry Markham--nineteen and green--must find a way tokeep his platoon alive. Here we see the stories Larry cannot bring himself totell--of friends who made the ultimate sacrifice in a war their countryscorned.\n\n&amp;quot;The Names of the Dead&amp;quot; is the story of a man trying to find his way backto himself--a story about storytelling and memories that refuse to fade. It isthe story of a man rediscovering the courage to love one woman, and, throughher, the world, his country, his family, and finally himself.&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;a At nightfall, the wing-song shrill of crickets announces that&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;span id=\"freeText2143512762834726084\" style=\"display:none\"&gt;&amp;quot;The air cools to crisp, carries sound farther. Last pears ripen and fall, ferment on the ground; the aroma of their wine mixes with the pungency of leaf smoke from nowhere and everywhere. At nightfall, the wing-song shrill of crickets announces that this season has a natural pathos to it, the brief and flaming brilliance of everything at the climax of life moving toward death.“October Brown had named herself for all of that.&amp;quot;So begins this beautifully written coming-of-age story about a young woman who struggles to overcome her family’s frightening legacy and keep her own child from similar emotional harm.It is 1950 and October Brown is a twenty-three-year-old first-year teacher thanking her lucky stars that she found a room in the best boardinghouse for Negro women teachers in Wyandotte County, Kansas. October falls in love with an unhappily married handyman, James Wilson, but when she becomes pregnant, James deserts her. Stunned, and believing that James will eventually come back to her, October decides to have the baby. But he doesn’t come back. As her reputation suffers, and with her job in jeopardy, she spends her days in self-deception and denial. Her best friend, Cora, contacts October’s family: her older sister, Vergie, and her aunts Frances and Maude, who raised the sisters after their mother was killed by their father.October goes back to her family in Ohio and gives birth to her son. Numb, she gives the child–David–to Vergie and her husband to raise as their own, then returns to Kansas City to rebuild her life. But something is missing–and, apparently too late, October realizes what she has done.What follows is the heartrending account of October’s efforts to reclaim her dignity, her profession, and her son, efforts that lead her into a bitter struggle with her sister and a confrontation with her parents’ violent past. The Midwest, the flourishing of modern jazz, and the culture of segregation form a compelling historical backdrop for this timeless and universal tale of one person’s battle to understand and master her own desires, and to embrace the responsibilities and promise of mature adulthood. October Suite plays a beautiful, haunting melody, turning everyday life into exceptional art.&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;a data-text-id=\"2143512762834726084\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent($(this));; When her first short-story collection was published in 1941, it heralded the arrival of a genuinely original writer who over the decades wrote hugely popular novels, novellas, essays, and a memoir, One Writer\'s Beginnings, that became a national bestseller. By the end of her life, Welty (who died in 2001) had been given nearly every literary award there was and was all but shrouded in admiration.In this definitive and authoritative account, Suzanne Marrs restores Welty\'s story to human proportions, tracing Welty\'s life from her roots in Jackson, Mississippi, to her rise to international stature. Making generous use of Welty\'s correspondence-particularly with contemporaries and admirers, including Katherine Anne Porter, E. M. Forster, and Elizabeth Bowen-Marrs has provided a fitting and fascinating tribute to one of the finest writers of the twentieth century.&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;a data-text-id=\"4379883503420332274\" But now Lily is a young woman and a mother herself, self-exiled from Hawaii but still attached to Anna\'s tragedy.     <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/43848.Bobbie_Ann_Mason">About Bobbie Ann Mason</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/43848.Bobbie_Ann_Mason"><img alt="43848" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1335388761p2/43848.jpg" class="right" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer4025852719321837894">Bobbie Ann Mason has won the PEN/Hemingway Award and was a finalist for the National Book Critics Circle Award, the American Book Award, and the PEN/Faulkner Award. Her books include <em>In Country</em> and <em>Feather Crowns</em>. She lives in Kentucky. </span>

    </div>
